RATING: 7.5
These guys are just so innovative. The problem I have with co-operative games is that they are co-operative. i.e. everyone works together to produ e the best plan. Space Alert gets round this problem by introducing a (difficult) time limit which means you can't afford to sit around and thin about things. however, i don't like games with time limits......
So the rating sort of reflects my admiration fro the game and the fact that I struggle with it.

RATING: 9.5
My favourite co-op so far by a big margin. A quick burst of frantic, exciting, puzzly fun. 

As a solo adventure: I deal out cards for 4 players separately as normal and try to co-ordinate between them with cards face-up. Although it has never failed to feel challenging (well, since the tutorials), after 10-12 games I found I was winning consistently with the white cards, so I shuffled the yellow ones in and lost three straight. That's more like it :D You have control over everything but piecing everything together in the time limit AND hand limitations gives it just the right level of crunch. I can see it being quite some plays before I win consistently enough to move onto yellows-only (and then adding the expansion!) 

As a group: I've only introduced 1 group to the tutorials so far but I must confess I took a slightly sadistic pleasure in their reactions to the extra layers that were gradually piled on. I love how you're contending with not only finding the right actions to take but communicating consistently so that you don't mess each other up. The static parts are GENIUS. And most importantly for me, I felt my solo experience did not detract from the experience of playing it as a group. In fact, I made at least one major blunder due to my lack of awareness of what others were doing (whereas before I would've worked it all out myself). 

A couple of nitpicks: I feel like you need to research the yellow threats beforehand as their effects are often not clear from the card alone. Example: Slime Y "Spread left (with 1 hit point)". The rulebook has a 4 paragraph explanation for this 6 word summary and I'll bet £10 it doesn't work how you'd think from the card alone. 
Also, the setup is a bit lopsided; maybe 5 mins setup, 10 mins play, 10 mins resolve, 5 mins pack away. I always play at least 3 games and don't mind at all, but I know it's a bug bear for some.

Bit of a wall of text there; I was pretty sure I'd like this game, but it easily exceeded my expectations. Love it!

edit: Upgraded to a 9.5 after getting the expansion, could easily rise to a 10

edit2: aaand it's a 10

RATING: 8
11/10/17 - Lowering to 8. We've kept on playing this one and while it's a great game, oh boy is it a crazy learning curve. Between plays we constantly forget rules and have to go over them. At seven plays, we're still learning rules. That's pretty crazy, given this is the same group that plays the very heaviest board games (Gallerist, Mage Knight, etc.).  So yes, I still think this is a great game, but only if you have a stable group that can take the time to really learn it. 

Enjoyable, tense game. It feels a lot like galaxy trucker (unsurprising given the same designer and them being made a year apart), although it is a significantly harder game and the rules do take a while to learn. The game is all about working as a team and coordinating actions. Once you get further into the game and get better as a team, planning for the random luck-element breakdowns becomes a bigger part of the game, A lot of things can throw plans off, so your team has to build in ways to work around those issues. Communication is a really big part of this game (telling people what cards you need, what you need during a data transfer, making sure you aren't duplicating errors, problem-solving). Great game and I would absolutely play it again & again, especially if I don't have to introduce new players to it. I know of other real-time games but there are none that feel exactly like this one, so like its Galaxy Trucker brother, it's definitely a refreshing pleasure to play.

RATING: 6
2009-01-12
Make sure that you have decent speakers connected to your CD-player.

2016-04-29
Space Alert tackles the most common and often the biggest problem in co-operative boardgames by removing the one dominant alpha player from the equation. There just is too much going on for one person to tell everyone what to do. Thus you truly have to work as a team made up of individuals. In my book this is the greatest achievement of the game.

But the space and time where you can actually play Space Alert is very limited. First, you need a decent stereo system to play the CD. Second, there cannot be any disturbances around. Thus basically you only can play at home behind closed doors.

The game is fun but its limited playability refrains me from rating it higher.

RATING: 8
Initial rating.  There are many things working at the same time here: cooperation, sci-fi, programmed movement, real-time, complicated combat.  It will take a few more plays to see if the game will hold up, but it seems promising right now...

Upgraded to an 8.  A truly unique boardgaming experience.  Its closest comparison is to RoboRally, but even that is a distant cousin.  It is quite difficult to teach, but the designers put an incredible amount of effort into the rulebook to support teaching.  Kudos!

RATING: 8
A fun game that combines the programming of [GAMEID=18] with the coop of [GAMEID=30549].  The game comes down to cooperative planning under stress (time, multiple threats, sequences of actions).  The players lose only when they are unable to successfully address the presented threats.  Perfect information -- it's the timing and urgency that define the gameplay.

A lot of fun, particularly as the robotic voice of the narrator is guaranteed to interrupt and annoy other nearby games.  (Quantity of sarcasm in previous sentence may vary.)

5-player may be a bit too easy.  Space theme and implementation absolutely rock.  Fair bit of replay with the different enemies, difficulties and audio tracks, but I could see this losing its luster over time.  Overall a great game, but not one that I feel compelled to purchase.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Space Alert is fantastic! It's unbelievable how much tension and excitement this game can pack in those 10 minutes! Of all the real-time games I have played, this one is the best by far.

If you are searching for a strategic cooperative game, this is not the right choice. Space Alert is all about adrenaline, trying to puzzle out what to do while everyone around is panicking... it's all about action! It might not be as strategic as other games, but it does the one thing a good game should do: be fun! 

I also really like the fact, that the game doesn't take itself too seriously. Space Alert can deliver some great laughter, when everybody around is shouting like mad people or when you start resolving your actions and you see how bad you messed up. Those are just fantastic moments!

This game is quick, unbelievably exciting and also a proof of what a board game can do, when there's a great designer behind it. Just brilliant! I can see myself playing this game a lot in the future.

RATING: 10
Upon my first play of Space Alert, I thought it was a fun lark, and rated it a solid 8.5. It has since grown to become both my favourite co-op, AND my favourite game over all! And I am NOT a sci-fi fan!
While playing, it is a tactical game, where decisions need to be made on the fly. The strategy comes in after the game [!!!], as players go through the post-mortem and make plans for the NEXT time they find themselves in a similar situation as what they have just faced.
My only real complaint is that it's quite the chore introducing noobs to the game.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Fun, fast and space. Need I say more? You try to save your space ship from meteroids, aliens, computer viruses and much more. The game takes ten minutes to play plus a resolution phase that takes around ten minutes as well. Real panic and the feeling of robo rally's "what the hell happened now?" Plus it's a real tough game which makes the (not that common) wins all the more enjoyable!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
:wow: What happened? :wow:

Three things that make this one of the best coop games ever:
1. There is no "one player tells everyone what to do" (though you do have to listen to the captain for coordination)
2. The scenario is always different, even when you play the same CD track.
3. You don't simply try to beat the game — you must [i]really[/i] cooperate and communicate clearly with your teammates... very tough!

And what differentiates Space Alert from other games? Real time reaction to a real time scenario. Sweet.

I don't like coop games as much as competitive games, and circumstances are usually not right for this one to hit the table.

RATING: 8.7
A fun game with some learning difficulty, but once your players know how to play, games can come and go very quickly.  You plan your moves in a robo-rally type card placement, and you all work together to combat incoming threats to your ship.  You will have to time your moves out well on the turn track in order to destroy the more difficult enemies.

Cons: This game takes a decent amount of time to teach for the full rules and the players will be confused during the first couple plays.  This means that each time you introduce it to new players (which I do a lot since I have a fairly large group that changes who comes each game day) you will have to be very patient that the first 1.5 hours will be teaching, learning, and testing.

Pros: After that, the fun begins.

Semi-Con: Since it is a co-op game, you are playing against an enemy with a plan laid out to you.  Each game plays like a puzzle and all players have to understand how to solve it to win.  My Fiancee and I have trouble getting into co-op games, since they are not as dynamic, and once a good strategy is determined, the game cannot compensate.  (We prefer to play against other humans)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Pros
- Real time is fun
- Coordinating with teammates is fun
- The theme makes sense and is intense
- The game total takes about 30 minutes
- Good sense of teamwork
- No alpha player/quarterbacking for a co-op

Cons
- It takes a long time for someone to grasp the game
- Works best when played with the same people instead of teaching new players over and over 
- Need a CD player/iPhone app
- Not so much real time thinking as it is about a puzzle
- Everybody has to understand the game completely and all it's nuances for you to be successful

Sold/Traded because:
- Too many new people being introduced to the game, it's hard to get past the training missions
- The puzzle part of the game starting standing out more and more and made less thematic sense

RATING: 6.5
Ouch, a 6.5? 

I really wanted to like Space Alert more than I do. First let down were the overly complex rules that make explaining it to a group a chore. The second lament came when I realized this is a 4 player only game -- lower player counts have you playing with surrogates. 

I've only played a handful of scenarios, and those were solo and with a few mates (two player each time). A few liked the idea, but it didn't feel like the game was humming along (the learning curve was never crested). 

I hope to introduce this to my core group sometime soon, but I often forget I still own it.

RATING: 8
As an exercise in teamwork, Space Alert is awesome fun.  Trying to coordinate your actions on board a starship under attack, with limited actions, multiple threats and playing to a soundtrack that controls the mission is both stressful and immensely enjoyable.  Trying to coordinate actions and plan your response to each threat is frenetic as you only have limited cards and time.  Once the frantic planning phase is done, you stop and step through the mission review, revealing cards to see whether you were all able to work together - most it ends with the ship being destroyed and death for the hapless crew, but it's so enjoyable to watch those desperate plans go awry because of one simple mistake.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
As of now I am sort of in love with Space Alert.  I can't stop thinking about it.  I've yet to play one of the "full missions" (with things like internal threats, advanced threats, and stuff like that), but so far every game I've played has been unique, challenging and a total blast. 

Like Galaxy Trucker, Chvatil creates something that is both familiar yet ambitious and mixes together a sort of "perfect storm".  Space Alert creates a frantic and tense atmosphere that can't be matched.

My only problem with the game so far is that at times the ending can feel a bit like a let down after the excitement of the initial "planning" portion. The goal of simply "surviving" a mission is not all together satisfying and the "scoring" system for rating your successes feels tacked on and incomplete.

However, these are small complaints that should not distract you from sitting down with this game for a romantic evening of laser-filled, alien-zapping, chaos.

**update**
having now played my first full mission I will say this:  I love Space Alert.

RATING: 8
A szerző nem okozott csalódást, ez egy szuper játék, a kooperatív műfajban amiket ismerek, azok közül talán egyedül a BSG veszi fel vele a versenyt, bár nincs sok közös pont a két játékban, talán csak az, hogy mindkettő stresszes (de nagyon más okból:)).
A játékszabályt olvasva (és utána játszva is) 2 játékra asszociáltam: Roborally (akciók tervezése) és Galaxis Roncsderbi (a szerző és gondolom a grafikus is ugyanaz, meg a téma is hasonló). Szerencsére ennyiben ki is merül a hasonlóság. A két említett játék nem nagy kedvencem (bár parti kategóriában nem rosszak végül is), ez viszont kifejezetten tetszett!
A játék pozitívumai: Először is rengeteg jó ötlet, ahogy a szerzőtől megszoktuk. Ebben a témában is sikerült megvalósítania, hogy minden mindennel összefüggjön. Rendkívül nagynak tűnik az újrajátszhatóság (röppályák, sok féleképp variálható fenyegetés kártyák, stb.) Felhasználóbarát az 5 féle nehézségi szint, és az ehhez készített kézikönyv (a szabálykönyvbe bele se néztem, a kéziköny egyértelmű). Van a játékban kihívás! Egy parti max fél óra! A kezdő (1.szint) ill. a haladó (2-5. szint) pályákon fixen 7 ill. 12 perc az esemény kör, a megoldás még negyed óra kb. Sok helyen hallottam, hogy cd kell hozzá, emiatt fokozottan nyelvfüggő, stb. szerencsére kaptam egy házilagos magyar fordítást, ez alapján tökéletesen fel lehetett készülni, és annyira minimális az angol (kb. 10 féle utasítás van), hogy nem szabad megijedni, főleg ha van akár 1 ember a társaságban, aki ért angolul. Egyébként hatalmas ötlet szerintem ez a fajta CD-s játékvezérlés.
Negatívum: Hangulatban azért a BSG jobb. Emellett nagyon nehéznek érzem a játékot, mi már a 2. szinten is simán kikaptunk háromból háromszor, igaz csak 3 fővel próbáltuk, úgy nehezebb, mint többen (az androiddal is kellett bíbelődnünk). Nagyon nehézzé teszi a szigorú időlimit, de épp ettől lesz tervezhető a játék hossza, és épp ezért vannak különböző szintek. 
Mindenkinek ajánlom, akinek gyorsan pörög az agya (az enyém ma nem pörgött elég gyorsan:)), nagyon egyedi és szórakoztató!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Vlaada Chvatil is the most innovative and talented designer of the last years. I probably wrote some variation of this sentence in a half a dozen comments already...

Space Alert is the only one cooperative game I thoroughly enjoy: is FAST and FURIOUS when you try to coordinate the actions with other players in a Roborallyesque first part of the game, while you're planning your actions in spite of the many crazy things that the game throws in your face.
But if the planning part is FUN, the resolution launches laughter in orbit, especially when you discover some team member screwed up big time, and the whole mission fails in a spectacular way for someone else's incompetence!

The game is so innovative and different, and it surely needs the right group of players: both experienced and willing to be not-so-serious!
I luckily know more than handful of guys with these qualities, so Space Alert will hopefully see lots of play, now that we have discovered its awesomeness!

Vlaada for the win, again!

RATING: 10
Space Alert is a fantastic game, because it is all about figuring out the most efficient way to operate as a team under a time crunch to mitigate both the risks that you can anticipate and the chaos that you cannot. And the thing about chaos? It's fair. When something goes wrong, you can't really be mad at anyone but yourself. When you fail a mission, it's usually because you failed to account for everything. You didn't do your math perfectly, or you didn't read the special ability of the enemy, or you forgot to pull energy from the reactor and ran out of fuel. But the game isn't impossibly difficult; it gives you ample time to account for error. You just have to give up the idea that you can perfectly account for everything that will happen.

You cannot control. You can only prepare.

RATING: 7
This game is a blast, once you finish your first mission. The learning curve is steep, and the tutorial book, despite its best efforts, doesn't help. Past that, though, once the game starts to make sense, it's actually a very sharp game of movement, management, and making sure everyone is synchronized.

One other note: I think the game is hampered by its attempts at comedy in the rulebook. The gameplay isn't funny, and the tone of the rulebook tends to get everyone in the mindset of playing a wacky game of bumbling space cadets, when you actually need to think as a focused, unified crew to succeed.

Unfortunately (as noted by a friend) the biggest problem is that you really need a group who will play several times so you can get to the "real" missions. After several plays, I realize my concern noted above is not the case.  The game is pretty simple to teach and quick to play.  Usually we play about 5 games in one sitting; the tutorial, a similuation, and then we jump to the real thing.  This game is crazy fun, and may go up to a 9.

RATING: 10
An absolutely awesome romp through space.  The programmed movement is stressful especially with the audio computer distractions.  Everyone rushes to make group decisions, but no one really knows for sure if it's going to work.  The walk-through of how the events actually transpired is some of the most entertaining moments I have ever experienced in a game.  It's hilarious watching moments where people think they did one thing and actually they did the other.

That is the genius of this game in my opinion.  Unlike any other cooperative game, I can have so much fun seeing what happened, even when nothing goes right.  You spend 10 minutes programming what you'll do, but after that it's all out of your control and I can't help laughing when people go running into walls and firing cannons that don't have power, etc..  And what makes it even funnier is that sometimes, despite all these horrible mistakes, you'll still attain a victory.  A superior game playing experience that is threatening to become my favorite game of all time if I could play it more.

RATING: 9.5
I love the chaos and play style of this game.  We always feel overwhelmed, and when things go wrong we're always laughing like mad fools.  A unique experience and a game I'm hoping to get to the table often!

A totally brilliant game, I've never played anything like it before!  After 24 plays it still feels very fresh and challenging.  A clear winner in the co-op games war!

Space Alert is a difficult co-op game all about attempting to tame chaos in real time. The players need to work together to survive 10 minutes in hostile space, being attacked by external threats, internal threats and malfunctions on a ship that just seems to be designed to not survive the encounter! You plan your actions in phases in time to a real time audio track that tells you when and where things happen, then you use cards from the appropriate decks to find out what exactly is going on in that area. This gives the game a ton of replayability and since there are decks with tougher encounters included, once you get good at the game you can up the challenge level. We have a great time playing this, with a lot of laughter when we're making mistakes during the resolution portion of the game, and I find that there is so much going on while the game is being played that it avoids one of the major pitfalls of just about every other co-op I've ever played: one player cannot simply dictate orders to every other player. Since everyone has to work together and communicate (except during the communications breakdowns!) and manage the action cards that they have, it does not devolve into just doing what the one player tells you to do. The rulebook is hilarious and I love the game!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I realize subsequent plays may increase this score for me, but as of now with my limited game time I have absolutely no desire to replay in hopes I like it more. There just isn't enough here in the first place to get me to want to play it again.

RATING: 7
I'll start by saying that I don't generally like co-op games.  I find them all a little contrived, and really dislike how the "quarterbacking" element leads to people feeling puppet-stringed by the most experienced player.

Initial Rating 7, which for me on a co-op game is like a 10.

RATING: 3
Is this a game, or a job?  Like screaming and yelling, and feeling like a schmuck when you mess up?  I'm pretty ambivalent about this game right now - that is, after having played it multiple times, but still not with all the rules yet, and only with 2.  It is hard - very hard.  We got it because of all the novelty in the design, and Ashar is into space themed stuff.  And I must say, the design is great, truly innovative, at least for a co-op.  My problem, though, is that there is little satisfaction here.  It's a screaming brain burn for 10 minutes, and then a slow unraveling of pain and mistakes for 20 minutes.  All of that is quite interesting, but fun?  VERY not sure yet.  I think it simulates the theme well, but maybe real-time warfare is not for me.  I enjoy games for the leisurely pace and the ability to contemplate, the satisfaction that comes from a well-played game, or from seeing new ways to victory.  Space Alert denies such pleasures, as the timing element makes for assured sub-optimal play, and then the unraveling of actions makes one feel very impotent.  While you can see what you can do better, you also kind of don't care once you've done so poorly.  So far, the kind of game that is objectively interesting and exciting, but when actually played and not just viewed from a distance, this one hurts.  Maybe I just don't like co-ops (except for Arkham Horror).  We'll see.  So far, though, this one hasn't generated too many warm fuzzies.

RATING: 7
UPDATE: OK, maybe not quite. This is a clever game, but the practical obstacles - getting a group of comparable experience to get over the non-trivial learning curve and play and grow together - are simply too large in practice. ---
UPDATE: This is a rare game for which I've had to substantially upgrade my rating. I don't know if it'll hold, but I've turned the corner and come to appreciate it more. Space Alert is definitely flawed from the narrative angle - it's more cooperative problem solving than a real cooperative game per se, in that the narrative and tension management aren't great, at least not at the more moderate difficulty levels. But the problem solving is fun, and the atmosphere is good.

It does have a fairly brutal learning curve on it, though. Figure at least 5 missions to become competent. That's not a *huge* time investment at 15 minutes a mission, but that's just to become competent and get to the point that the game is fun, and everyone has to put in the time, not just most of the players - a new player on an experienced team is along for the ride and not likely to feel like they contributed anything. Like all Chvátil's stuff, it's overly chromy, tricky to teach, and more obscure than it needs to be, which is unfortunate. But once, you've got it, you've got it.

The other unfortunate problem is that any given instance of the game can be completely felled by an accidental misplayed card or misunderstanding. It shouldn't happen but it's easy to let your guard down and make a simple error. I almost wonder if the game is best played with the cards face up, just to avoid frustrating misplays that ruin the game. It doesn't seem like hiding the played cards is accomplishing very much.

But still, even with its quirkiness and fragility, I am rather fond of it and hope I can add it as a third genuinely successful cooperative game, after Lord of the Rings and Pandemic. But no question it'll appeal to a much narrower audience than either of those games.
---
Deeply disappointing. Lacks tension, thematically quite weak. An exercise in puzzle-solving and not risk management, and so almost completely lacks excitement. Also, like all other CGE games, it's over-complex given what it is. It takes 15 minutes to explain a 10 minute game, and that kind of ratio combined with time pressure makes the game incomprehensible on initial play. Also, the game is horribly susceptible to bad group dynamics in a way that games like Pandemic and Lord of the Rings aren't. Also, Red Alert has a critical flaw for a cooperative game: the most effective strategy is to pick one person to just do everything. It doesn't really have enough complexity for having multiple people look at the problem and giving their own opinions to be all that beneficial. So it seems mostly about minimizing communication friction rather than actual group problem-solving - i.e., just having one person tell everyone what to do. I want to give it another chance - there is some chance a few more plays will improve my impressions - but first impressions were a big disappointment after the wonderful Galaxy Trucker, and my rating is a lot more likely to go down than up I think.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Updated 11/6/10.

Space Alert is a game that I've only had the pleasure of playing on a few occasions.  My wife despises cooperative games, so it is not welcome in our house.  On those occasions, however, I loved, loved, loved this game.  

I remember reading the concept and thinking that Space Alert would be little more than a space-themed version of Scene It - an unnecessary technological distraction in a game that may or may not stand on its own merit.  What I got was an awesome, chaotic, thrilling experience.  

Space Alert fits (broadly) into the Agricola archetype -- it is a game that I enjoy because of the desperate struggle against the system-imposed (as opposed to player-imposed) constraints.  Generally, my enjoyment of games in this archetype comes from the strain of complicated mental gymnastics as I attempt to puzzle out a solution.  Space Alert, however, is satisfying in a completely different way -- the frenetic pace of the planning stage is a rush in itself, despite the relatively simple programming tasks players are expected to perform.  Watching the plan put into action (usually with very unexpected results) was also extremely enjoyable (even where the plan failed, it was more amusing than frustrating).  I find this implementation of Vlaada's two-phase system miles more entertaining than the similarly structured Galaxy Trucker.

As a cooperative game, I also find it to be a significant improvement over the higher-rated Pandemic.  Here, the planning phase is fast and frantic, generating a thematically-appropriate panic.  Appropriately, success comes from trying to suppress your panic and to think and communicate clearly.  Because of the chaotic context, everyone is called upon to do what they can where they can to the best of their judgment, and there is usually neither a dominant personality, nor an implied judgment.  In Pandemic, the system unfolds on a turn-by-turn basis, giving players plenty of time to logically plan out their joint solution.  Additionally, Pandemic's epidemic system is counter-thematic: throughout the game, players come to know that the most-infected cities are no longer a threat and other, possibly uninfected, cities are upcoming.  This is not read-and-react to a swiftly unfolding disaster; this is communally deconstructing a game system and exploiting the underlying math.  Because this is more calculable and puzzle-like, it is prone to having a single, strong personality dictate group decisions, or, in the alternative, to have the group unhappily critique an individual player's sub-optimal decisions.  The result is marginalizing, and not my idea of fun.

With additional levels of complexity that I haven't even touched and the option of conducting multi-mission campaigns, Space Alert has the potential to continue to entertain me for a very long time.  It really scratches a unique gaming itch, and does so without ever leaving me feeling unsatisfied.  

[b]My #3 Favorite Game[/b]

RATING: 8
One of the detractions for me was the user guide. I know a lot of people praise it for being really funny, and some of it is, but sometimes the "comedy" is a male instructor picking on the female cadets or trying to sleep with them. These "jokes" made me wince, not laugh.

The game itself, though, is free from that.

What it is not free of is lots of little fiddly rules, so many that they actually teach you the game in parts, and the need to play with  at least four people.

When I played it, the game was fun. We parcel out the roles and people say I've got it and they they try to do it but it's hard to make sure that everything is timed well with the other players (a lot of actions need to be taken in sequence or at the same time to be effective).

However, I don't regularly play with four or five, I usually play with two or three, so getting together irregularly with four or five means going through the complicated rules every time, which is quite long, and we don't actually get good at the game, and if you don't get good at the game, well dying over and over again is only funny for so long considering the prep time to play this game.

So, it's probably very good for a dedicated group of four or five players, but since that isn't me, it just collected dust. Even when I was four or five it didn't necessarily get pulled out, either because of time issues, or fatigue (those rules are really complicated), or because people were interested in a specific game that wasn't Space Alert (I own many games competing for the four or five players slot).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[b]Includes Space Alert: The New Frontier.[/b]

Rated "9" after one solo play. I ran through this solo to prepare to teach the game to my friends. I smuggly played all my cards, moving four people around the ship, and then sat back admiring my work. Then, I replayed the events, watched all my bumbling mistakes, and barely survived as the ship was pummeled to within one point of surviving.

I can't think of a more inventive game. This is completely unique, with a sense of humor, real tension, and a fantastic rulebook that teaches the game in stages. I need to play this with 4 to 5 people to get an "official" rating, but I can't think of any other game that I've been so eager to try.

Update: Wow. Played this for the first time as a whole group, running through two trial sessions. Ouch. I think we felt collectively stunned and bludgeoned. There's a lot going on, and it requires some serious savviness to teach this to a group. Thankfully, the rulebook and tutorials try to make this as easy as possible. I think the group reaction was a mixture of dazed but willing to try this again. More plays will determine if this has staying power in the group.

Update: Took quite some time between plays, but finally got this back on the table. Five of us went from the simplest trial run to full blown internal threats and all the various actions. This is simply the most unique game I own. It's also the most exciting blend of theme and mechanics. I just love it.

The only thing that keeps me from giving this a 10 is that I can't say I'll always be playing this. You need the right crowd, and they need to share the same enthusiasm to keep it hitting the table with regularity. This game requires dedication... someting beyond the "well I'm glad I tried this" response. If that's the overall crowd reaction, this game won't get replayed.

I'm hoping I have a few fellow gamers willing to continue to investigate this one, as I'm fascinated by the pressures that the game applies, and I really want to beat the system! If I play this game only one more time, it will have been worth every penny.

RATING: 9
I bought this game mainly because it was said to offer a good solo experience. It turned out to offer a GREAT solo experience!

Solo, I tend to win very often, planning carefully and keeping a good overview. This feels like a captain giving detailed orders to a crew (of androids in this case) and keeping the ship intact.

As a multiplayer experience, there simply is mayhem! People have no idea what someone else is doing most of the time, they perform counter-productive actions and mess up each other's plans because of it. The additional effects (like disruption of communications) add to the challenge. I tend to lose multiplayer games, but it is really a lot of fun!

Not for you if you dislike programmed actions or if you are a sore loser who cannot laugh when losing a game because someone else messed up. The people I played with laughed and shuddered at their own and others' mistakes and we had a great time! You really need to play this with people who will do their best but who do not care if it all falls apart anyway - or solo, for a nice puzzle game, really.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
The game has a nice theme and pretty solid art and components. The premise is fairly straight-forward: players are hanging out on a space ship with 2 decks and 3 rooms each. In each room, there are big buttons that do things like charge the shields, and fire the guns at the various space beasties that are attacking. Guns are often too weak to kill the space critters, so firing in cooperation with the other players is often critical. Energy can run out quickly, so someone has to take care of recharging stuff lower in the ship. The goal is to survive the onslaught (primary) and take trophies (secondary).

What will probably make or break the game for the average person is the way actions are carried out, which is via programmed actions on individual player boards, and which is done "secretly," but with each player being allowed to tell everyone else what it is that they're doing. Because of the timing required for all of the moves, the table quickly becomes a mish-mash of questions, answers, and card placements. What generally ends up occurring as a result during the actions is entertaining gaffes which cascade and create all sorts of problems further down the line. It's sort of amusing, but it's also annoying to me in that making an effective plan almost requires someone giving orders to the table, or at least some sort of system because it is way too chaotic to deal with input from 4 other people at once about what they're planning to do and what they have in their hands. 

For some reason I want to liken it to a cocktail party from hell: 4 people are trying to talk to you at once while an out of control DJ is barking out nonsense through his microphone, which is annoying everyone in the room. Meanwhile the exterior of the building is being ravaged by wild animals which the guests are occasionally taking potshots at with their assault rifles.

Programmed action games are not generally my bag, and I dislike Space Alert with particular vehemence because there is so much specific communication required to do anything effectively, and yet 5 players are required which makes adequately communicating everything with the whole table incredibly frustrating. Playing with fewer would do little to clear the muddle, since then players are assigned multiple boards, which increases the amount of things to mentally process. Not my thing, at all.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Very difficult game to rate. I almost bought it unplayed, simply because it's from Vlaada, and I adore his recent games. But I have not had great success with co-ops, even though in my mind I feel like I am open to the idea. E.g. I bought Shadows, didn't like it and sold it. I bought Pandemic, didn't like it, and still have it. But for Vlaada. Given that Space Alert is also a little pricey, I convinced myself to at least wait and try before buying. And I'm glad I did.

I see why manny people rave about the game. The game play is MUCH more involved than your typical co-op. But for me it's too involved. That is, I'm ok with programming games (e.g. Robo Rally, and I especially like Himalaya), but my brain has a limited capacity. I can pretty much process exactly what [i]I'm[/i] going to do, over 5 stages, and maybe guess what someone else might do so far as it might affect me. But in Space Alert the group needs to keep track of what [i]everyone[/i] is going to do, through [i]12[/i] stages. And with the timer discussions are necessarily frantic. I was told that the game is best with 5 players, and maybe it is (it's what I played with in my one playing), but purely based on social dynamics, I think I would prefer frantic group discussions limited to 3 players.

I will say again that for people who genuinely like co-ops, can deal with massive amounts of planning, this knocks it out of the park. I am still very impressed with Vlaada, even though this one isn't for me personally. The variety and creativity of working together is really head and shoulders above other co-ops. In fact, I'm too lazy, but after playing this, I shuold probably go back and lower my rating for other co-ops. Lord of The Rings somehow stands alone, however, in co-ops I actually like, at least when played with private cards so there's personal responsibility.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
The presentation at Spiel 2008 was a letdown for me: the computerised voice made everything difficult to understand for no good reason at all save the creation of atmosphere. There are much better ways of achieving this, but presumably there was a tight budget to adhere to.

Forward to 2009, when I played my first tutorial mission during the presentation of Space Alert to the Dutch public at the Spellenspektakel. I was briefly explained the rules&mdash;which are not that involved&mdash;and off we went. We managed to save the ship, but I will not play another game. There are several reasons. [u]First[/u], the planning phase is the funniest, as you're cooperating and talking and planning and what-not. Compared to the clean programmatical efficiency of the replay phase, it shoots the latter clear out of the water even though you need the feedback. [u]Second[/u], and far worse: the action cards which do nothing but hinder the players to force them to come up with bizarre solutions to a situation which involves nothing more complex than going to the next room and pressing a button. All sorts of micromanagement involved (energy levels, damage control, etcetera) is the kind of thing you expect a computer to do. I get the distinct impression that in the background story the astronauts decided to make saving the ship a challenge by using only their left hand and speaking a little known dialect of Klingon even the Klingons have trouble with. Unfortunately removing these cards guts the games quicker than a Tyrannosaurus rex guts his next meal. [u]Third[/u], the game is meant for groups who play sufficiently often to get to know their quirks and strong and weak points. If you consider this a game to be played occasionally, you'd be best off by not buying it at all, methinks.

All in all: a lot of creative game design, so make sure you try it at least once&mdash;but don't be surprised if the game doesn't live up to your expectations. It certainly didn't meet mine.

RATING: 9.6
Absolutamente fantástico. Me parece el juego cooperativo definitivo, y incluso jugándolo a single player es una auténtica maravilla. Eso si, la expansión es 100% obligatoria, sobretodo por el sistema de experiencia y especializaciones. Hacen que el juego sea... simplemente brillante, le da el aliciente para jugar, y volver a jugar. Aunque mucha gente lo teme, este juego no se hace repetitivo para nada, pues aunque solo haya 8 tracks, las cartas se roban aleatoriamente, y las partidas nunca son iguales. Aunque si no te convence, hay generadores de misiones aleatorias, así que no hay excusa. El juego base incluye 2 niveles de dificultad, cosa que se agradece porque al modo fácil le terminas pillando el truco enseguida, mientras que en normal (amarillo) las cosas se complican de una manera interesante.
Recomendado 110%, si llego a saber que era un juego así, no hubiera esperado a comprarlo en español. Para mi, el juego del año (y estamos a 3 de enero xD), grande Vlaada.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Components: The board is beautiful and fun to move about in. The threat cards have lavish art and functional iconography. The robots and rockets are cool. Though the action cards are simplistic, I'm impressed with how clearly they function (e.g. the ship is colored corresponding to the direction of the arrow). All components are evocative of the theme. When this game is set up, it looks like fun is about to happen.

Experience: Awesome. We usually fail our scenarios, unless we play through the exact same scenario again, which is still fun. When unknown threats are appearing (both outside the ship and inside), it is such a mind boggle to try to keep track of where we are during which turn, whether we have the juice for another laser blast, whether a certain threat has been properly neutralized, etc. Often, playing out the pre-programmed actions can be just as exciting as programming them, because we often don't know whether we've succeeded or not. Very rich in theme.

At times a player is left with nothing in particular to do, but even being in this role myself I still have fun, because I feel like I'm on standby during a tense situation. Very chaotic as players try to communicate to each other different bits of information; feels like an entire team in the hot seat trying to respond to an emergency.

This game is a rarity among cooperatives, in that more brains are better than fewer. Yes, it's more difficult to coordinate with more players, but there is too much information for me to be able to successfully keep track of all by myself.

Playing this game requires real logistical skill, so it's difficult to be competent unless we play several times in succession and get into a groove.

First Played: 2010-03-10
Last Played: 2013-03-26
Eagerness to Play Again: Soon

Plays (19):
3 players: 17
4 players: 2

RATING: 9
This is an incredible experience game.  Programming actions (7 during training, 12 on regular missions) under time pressure, with random threats appearing from without and within as dictated by the soundtrack for the mission.  It has the programming of Robo-Rally, but also a lot of the feel of Galaxy Trucker (do stuff under time pressure, then see how everything turned out).  And it's cooperative.  There are a number of features that can turn people off:  cooperation, time pressure, programming actions; but if you're OK with all that, I'd bet you'll like this one.

RATING: 7
This game is so frustrating. Well, that's not true. Owning this game is so frustrating! Unless you have a set group of people play this game regularly, you won't get the full use out of it. Everytime you pull this out, you spend 20 minutes (or more) explaining the rules to everyone, then you go through a training mission and everyone enjoys it. Then you have to spend another 5-10 minutes explaining more rules, then you do another mission and everyone is happy, then you spend another 5-10 minutes explaining MORE rules, then you play another mission, then you start explaining MORE RULES and everyone says they've had enough. Rinse, repeat, 3 times or more and you'll be sick of this game. I just want a space crew!

Also, if you play the training missions with 5 players, the game is waaaaaay too easy. We had a single player play no more than 2 cards in all 7 of his actions because there just wasn't anything we needed him to do. My suggestion is to sit out if there are 5 players so 4 people can experience the frenzy. It's also more fun to watch your friends struggle through their first few missions instead of you barking orders at them.

I'd also like to have the training missions setup in baggies so I don't spend the first few minutes refamiliarizing myself with the rules and refamiliarizing myself with the setups for the training missions.

RATING: 6.5
I must admit that this is my least favorite Chvátil design (although I adore his others). It's basically "[gameid=18] meets [gameid=31481]" and whether that sounds interesting to you or not, will most likely dictate whether you will like this game. 

For a 10-minute game, I think 27 total pages of rules is excessive. Granted, you don't have to read every section, but still. The conversational, sometimes humorous, style in which the tutorial rulebook is written wore thin very quickly for my group, but the same may not hold true for yours. 

The CD gimmick seems to be just that. It's basically a sand-timer with voice-over from the ship's computer. While the CD is playing, people work together in real-time to combat potential threats to the ship that are drawn from a deck. This is done using a hand of cards that can be played as movement to different areas of the ship or actions, such as fire laser, raise shields, and power the energy cores. 

Please don't avoid the game, based on my comments, particularly since I haven't played it enough yet. It all seems pretty clever, really, but for me, it was just not very engaging. With the right group, it could be a ton of fun, but I'll be busy playing [gameid=38980], thank you very much. ; )

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Players are crew members of a Sitting Duck class galactic survey ship cooperating and trying to keep the ship intact while the computer performs an automated 10 minute scan of the region.  Players discuss and program their actions using cards during the frantic 10 minutes, while listening to a CD track representing the computer.  The computer, while scanning the region, warns players of incoming threats, comms. blackouts, and so on.  Player actions are limited to moving between zones in their ship, or pushing buttons to activate systems (energy distribution, firing weapons, ancillary systems).  When the CD track ends, the actions are resolved to see if the ship survived intact.  Feels a bit like the last 10 minutes of your typical Star Trek episode, with bad dudes coming in, not enough energy for the shields, reactor's stressed, etc.  Very clever cooperative game, some original stuff here, and difficult to defeat the game system.  The game gradually teaches you the ships systems, beginning with weapons and energy, and easing you into rockets, interceptors, battlebots and ensuring the computer screen saver doesn't come on (love that bit).  Tons of different threats and malfunctions, and seven different "threat trajectory" templates, ensures there is a lot of replayability.  This is the third Vlaada Chvatil game I've played, and yet again, it's different and excellent.  A great set of rulebooks present the concepts in cadet training "lecture" format, and it's a funny read.  Excellent components.  It's not for everyone, but I love the originality, and am mainly playing it solitaire.  More from Vlaada, please!  Eight games in, and I'm still in the simulators - the game won't trust me with a real ship yet.

CASELL-COMPTE
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.25
This is a pretty simple game in a lot of ways.  Just have to program out a dozen actions using cards, and your only options are move (left, right, up, down) and act (A, B, C).  But somehow, it's not an easy one to explain.  Too many things going on; too many possibilities for what those actions do; too many enemies with different scripts that you're figuring out how to fight.
No game better drives home the necessity for good leadership: for a commander who understands what's going on and can issue orders clearly and concisely.  They have to make sure there's enough power, that the crew are stationed around the ship, and most importantly, that the enemies are shot down quickly, before they have a chance to hurt you.
However, even if you don't have a great captain/crew and are just struggling along, the game can still be a lot of fun.  Even if you think things are going perfectly, it's possible that you missed something, and the execution phase can have some surprises in store.  Whether you're sailing through with flawless planning or crossing your fingers that your battered ship barely holds together, it's a good time.

One of the only real problems is the fact that you have to play in a quiet enough spot to hear the soundtrack, and that the quiet spot then becomes noisy once you start.  The other is the increasing difficulty/complexity of the game, once you move past the simplified tutorial mode into the main game, then add in the harder cards, then the expansion..  In order to play with these things, you kind of need a core group that plays this a minimum number of times a year.  If it sits on the shelf for too long, your skills will get rusty and you'll be starting from scratch when you pick up again.  Same if you're constantly teaching new players.  This means you won't have a chance to get better at it, and consequently will be missing out on a lot of the game.  I've still never played with the expansion, for this reason.  It just doesn't seem line the type of game people want to sink the time into, or feel like playing frequently enough to overcome this.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.36
Another Vlaada entry on my list.
Elect a player as a captain, one as a communication officer and one as a tactical expert and prepare for 10mins of frenzy. 

While things are happening in real time - due to an audio track playing (cd included or mp3 on CGE website, or even written sheets on BGG if you want to keep your own pace) - you are programming your character's moves and actions, doing your best to activate the cannons in time to deal with a threat while your crew mate powers them using the generators and another one manages to activate the defensive shields..

Only after the mission is over you evaluate what happened, and discover possible missteps, often leading to disastrous consequences.
It's short, cahotic, thinky and loud at the same time. A gem.
P.S.: I wouldn't ever buy a game with a CD audio in it if it wasn't Vlaada!

RATING: 7
Great little game if you want a real time puzzle.  You get a lot of information and have to try and figure out a solution before time runs out.  If you like logic puzzles it is near perfect.  

The complexity of the game makes it really easy to have one little mistake, like putting a card upside down, cause massive death.  

The downside seems to be that someone tends to direct traffic in order to get the best outcome, which could lead to some head butting.  Great test of teamwork skills.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Space Alert is a havoc-filled communication puzzle. Players must coordinate actions across multiple rounds, neutralizing various threats, and among other things, keeping the computer awake. There are a lot of plates to spin, and everyone needs to be both independent and united for the team to succeed. While this is all well and good, it fails to coalesce into a 'fun' experience. Its certainly interesting, but all of the programmed actions culminate in a disappointingly mechanical resolution. After resolving the various actions and treats you simply win or you don't, with very little fanfare. While a real-time cooperative space bridge game is certainly an innovative premise, the lack of an appropriate climax leaves much to be desired.

RATING: N/A
Few games have racked up such a string of enthusiastic accolades as Space Alert. The need for frantic cooperation under the stressful constraints of real-time pressure won't suit every gamer, and the chaotic feel and random elements do mean that some folks should just give this game a miss. But Space Alert creates a wonderfully immersive experience, and features mechanics that force players to work together in a suspenseful environment that has a genuine sense of panic which matches the theme perfectly. When played with a group of friends that share a common familiarity with the game, it offers an experience that few other games can create. The tense real-time pressure is enhanced with an audio soundtrack that adds a unique and creative element which many will appreciate and will want to come back to over and again. Vlaada Chvatil's creative genius is evident in his trademark warm humour, innovative mechanics capably serving the theme, and a compelling and memorable game experience. Games like this are a rare gem, and deserve all the praise they get. Every gamer should at least try it once.

Full review: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/1051753

Comments: RoboRally played cooperatively and on a clock.  Highly regarded co-op game, 4 or 5 players are essential, plays in 30 minutes, tense gameplay, real-time time pressure with audio adds a unique and creative element that many appreciate.  Negative comments remark on the amount of randomness, and some hatred for the audio CD, others don't like the stress of real-time play.  Is somewhat chaotic perhaps, but the theme is immersive and it should work well for teens who enjoy the challenge and the theme.

See: [filepage=56253][/filepage]

RATING: 2
I get the point is to create a bunch of noise and chaos so that things don't go well and you all have a good laugh about it, but … it's not that funny. Arranging action cards face down to melt rods and push buttons is -- yawn -- not exciting or laughable, so the game doesn't succeed for me in either sense. Oh, I know, people are tired of zombies but it would make the experience more immediate. Melting rods is supposed to be fun?

It's easier to express my dislike for the game now that Escape the Cursed Temple is out. That one is also a cooperative, real-time game with a soundtrack, but it succeeds for the way you frantically do something physical (roll dice) and share things among each other (the dice) to help one another. The physicality adds energy and tension, and the sharing adds a real element of cooperation, of helping and being helpless. I'll see you over there.

The more I play this game, the more I enjoy it.

RATING: 9
[2016 February 26; 24 plays; 9/10]
The mechanisms and theme come together at every point. There are of course complex rules which could be cut, but as with all of Vlaada's designs, the game is better for having them. As he is so masterful at doing, the "gamey" rules serve to compliment the theme and add to a complexity I find tasteful.
It's in [I]Space Alert[/I] that I feel Vlaada's video game developer roots really come through, and this only adds to its charm.

Many will brag that this game dispels all issues of bossy alpha-gamers, but I don't really agree. Some players will take charge, commanding others around the table to take care of threats in a specific manners, attempting to coordinate the entire team. Given how difficult Space Alert is to complete (and even more challenging to complete with a high score!), I've never been annoyed. Further, players must program their own moves, manage their own hand of cards, and should track their own resource use and player movement. Everyone may not equally coordinate every decisions, but everyone does have a lot of personal responsibility and this keeps the game very enjoyable!

[B]My favorite cooperative game.[/B]

RATING: 6
This is yet another box full of awesome from Vlaada.  The tutorial missions play quickly but ramp the difficulty up nicely, introducing enough new concepts each time to keep things interesting.

I've only played with five so far ... usually there was one person without a whole lot to do, but we only made it up through lesson five (ie we didn't do the advanced tutorial introducing internal threats, or a real full mission, obviously).

I think this game has potential for real-world use as a team-building exercise.

Just like in the real world, you have to communicate effectively and quickly and delegate responsibility and make sure everybody follows through, or else you'll all die together in the cold vacuum of space.

RATING: 2
Played 2 times with 4 and 5 players.

I don't even remember my first play of this game, but...

JUNE 2014 - I love some of the ideas in this game but hate the gameplay. Vlaada seems to be the king of soulless, overly fiddly euros. This is a wildly different game from Dungeon Lords, but I hate the intensely obvious euro-symmetry going going on. It doesn't help that I dislike real-time games (I feel most of them are best suited to video games), but Dungeon Lords isn't real-time, and I feel the same way about it.

I actually left a game night when this was what was staying on the table. It still suffers from Alpha player syndrome (actually, more like Alpha and Beta syndrome in my last playing), and I don't find the game nearly interesting enough to care. This ship is 5/2 move 2, that ship is 8/2 move 1, and move left move down use some action - bleh.

I'm done with Vlaada's games. Seriously. I don't have much time to game now that I'm older and I'm suck of wasting two hours of precious gaming time on his designs.

Want real-time? Play Speed.
Want Co-op battles? Play Lord of the Rings The Card Game.
Want programmed actions? Play Robo Rally.
Want sci-fi battles? Play Eclipse.

Nothing (and I mean nothing) about the gameplay works for me).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Pros
-Short game length (10min play+10min resolution)
-Fairly easy to teach (game includes several tutorial missions)
-Hilarious (best) rulebook I've read
-Innovative, real time and use of audio tracks
-Cooperative game where alpha player is almost a non-issue. It is almost impossible for a single player to micromanage due to real time aspect and hidden card info
-Reasonable difficulty level that is very scalable/customizable (especially with expansion). 
-Good variability on cards/enemies
-Good replayability despite drawing scenarios from fixed audio tracks (apps also provide randomized tracks)

Cons
-2 and 3 player modes feel tacked on. Game optimally requires 4 or 5 players
-Not all players will find chaos and plans backfiring entertaining
-Possibly repetitive if played a lot, or players start memorizing where enemies appear (highly unlikely)
-Could probably use slightly more variations of commands beyond "communications down", "incoming data", and "transfer data".
-Without expansion, no character powers between players (only 1 heroic action) and no real campaign mode/character growth (both added in expansion)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Fantastic, innovative game that's truly cooperative without artificial information hiding rules, and that requires coordination and communication. It's Galaxy Trucker plus Roborally, and I like the Galaxy Trucker-related art.

Duration: Each mission is around 10 minutes of intense cooperation and communication.
Design: Amazing that no one has done anything like it! The real-time nature of the game ameliorates the "bossy player" problem in co-op games.
Strategy: The way to get better is to have the group become familiar with the game and learn to communicate effectively.
Components: Colourful pieces with some Galaxy Trucker cameos. The limited missions on the CDs would be a problem without the community.
Theme: Even though you're just programming movements and activations, the real-time aspect of the game gives you a crisis feel.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very innovative cooperative programming game in real time. The real-time aspect cleverly avoids one of the pitfalls of other coop games, namely one player telling everyone else what to do. Here, you simply don't have enough time. Players program a number of actions in order to save the spaceship from attacking aliens or other such threats. Only when these steps get resolved after the end of the mission they will get to know whether they succeeded - and it is quite easy to send the ship to its doom by one careless move.

Planning your steps can be a hectic endeavour. Often you must endure being shouted at. In short, this is nothing for the faint-hearted. In fact, the game is also rather inaccessible which is unexpected in a coop. New players need to learn the ropes by going through the training missions which I suppose can get rather frustrating for experienced players. On the other hand, skipping the training would be rather frustrating for the newbies (and possibly for the veterans, too, due to all the stupid mistakes being made). I guess what all of this means is that to really get the full enjoyment out of this game you need to be playing repeatedly with the same group of people. You cannot just bring in a new player and launch into space right away.

When you do have that kind of group I can imagine this being lots of fun, though. I just wish the announcer's voice from the CD wasn't so terribly distorted. I guess in this case unfortunately theme won over function.

RATING: 6
Unfortunately, I walked away feeling rather "meh" about the whole experience. Space Alert didn't let me down for what I thought I would find: an interesting timer component and awesome theme. What I didn't care for was the combination of the puzzly nature of it all, and the chaos. I have experienced Chvatil's puzzle-like mechanic before in Dungeon Lords. While I find the DL system too complex to get any real enjoyment out of it, it is still a quality game and a well thought-out one. In fact, the puzzle aspect of it was unlike any other game I've played. SA also has this feel, but one mis-step from either you or an opponent means that everything you planned for is delayed by one round. This means that all the careful planning that went into the game gets fucked, and you just have to keep your fingers crossed that everything works out okay. I really didn't like this aspect; the whole first 10 minutes of planning is chaotic enough, but interesting for a co-op game. I felt like all our planning was for nothing. as a mis-step (easily made with all the chaos) delayed everything, and a Giant Octopus ripped our ship to shreds because of it. A little chaos keeps things interesting, but too much of it takes away from a big aspect of games that I like: the ability to control the system in the face of particular difficulty. SA was full of random chaos, and I didn't think there was any way to take control of it. I'm sure with repeated plays and a solid understanding of the game, one would do much better. But after my first experience, I don't care to get better at this one. Oh well!

RATING: 8
[b][i]Space Alert[/i][/b] is a great team game where a group works to keep a struggling spaceship alive for a real-time 10 minutes (the game has a recording sending waves of baddies your way). Strategy is not wide spread, at least not in a traditional sense. Instead, games are won or lost based on how well your team works together and communicates on vital details such as are there 4 or 5 energy in the main reactor. Expect to laugh as your space ship is destroyed by a Giant Octopus because [i]somebody[/i] mis-timed their cannon fire.

RATING: N/A

RATING: 8.5
My rating is subject to change after I play this game some more.

I generally dislike cooperative games.  Generally it is the more social people who want to play it and there is always the one guy (me) who knows the rules and needs to explain it and often knows the strategies.  So often, cooperative games turn into that one guy suggesting what everyone does or letting people fend for themselves and watching the game implode.  Neither option is fun for me.

Space Alert does not have that problem at all.  You just don't have time to micromanage people.  Maybe as I play it more, this will change.  I really hope not, because I really enjoy this game the few times I have played it.

I think the mechanics are good and I like the flavor that the rulebook puts into the game.  Something as small as the "ooops, I tripped" rule is fantastic and adds a lot to the game.

RATING: 7.5
I've played Space Alert a bunch of times now, and still have some unresolved feelings about the game. I admire it. But I go back and forth on the question of whether I actually enjoy it. Mostly, I do enjoy it. However, it's a difficult, stressful, often frustrating game. I guess that's both its weakness and its strength.

I definitely don't enjoy teaching the game to new players. There's a lot to explain, and it tends to befuddle new cadets. But with people who already know how to play, or have at least observed the game being played, it's a lot of fun.

I also find that the game is a hassle to set up and put away. That might seem like a dumb issue to raise, but it does keep me from suggesting it more often. There are a lot of little fiddly pieces and decks and tokens and markers and boards for such a short game.

Despite these issues, Space Alert is a captivating game that's unlike any other I've played. It's as much a sociology experiment as it is a game. In other cooperative games, there's the potential for one player to bully the others, trying to control all aspects of the game himself. This is simply not possible with Space Alert. The game is played in real time, and it won't be possible to execute a perfect plan. Each player will be scurrying around the ship trying to put out fires while trying to stay in communication with the rest of the team. Some coordination of effort is necessary, but there's not enough time to carefully map out everyone's moves. And inevitably, you'll screw something up or forget something or time your action incorrectly or run out of power or try to use a gravlift at the same time as someone else. Honestly, I think it's more fun to lose this game than to win.

So far, we've mostly played our cards face up on the action boards. Otherwise, it's too hard to keep track of what you're doing and where you are on the ship. As we get better, we might flip them upside down, but so far we prefer to keep them exposed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Work.
That's what this game feels like.
Work.
Not exciting "space-exploration" kinda work. More like civil-servant, office-politics "file this in triplicate" work.
I wanted this game for a LONG time, and couldn't wait to introduce it to my friends. We've only played the practice missions, and now it sits there on the shelf, a reminder of past mistakes.
Just like an illicit affair with that red-head from accounting, you'll find yourself regretting this.
Not that it doesn't try. I mean, just like that hottie red-head, it's got everything going for it; a great sense of humor, first and foremost. The rule book introduces you to the world of this game like a coy glance over coffee. It's sublime in how it attracts you with it's affectionate attitude, and quick-witted remarks, slowly luring you in by introducing mechanics and components, like toys in a secret "Red Room." After a few tentative plays, you'll find that you're trying to carve out time for this new excursion in your already hectic life, and you'll have trouble trying to convince your friends that your relationship with Space Alert is just harmless fun.
But they know.
They know it'll all end in a mug-throwing tantrum in the break room, and that you'll be standing there, with everyone looking at you, as you slowly realize you have to make that call to your spouse before someone else does, because everything is about to get worse...wait, where was I going with this?
Oh, yeah. This game is WORK. It's very funny, but you spend most of your time in the game getting things wrong, which isn't as funny as much as it's frustrating. You were in the wrong place at the wrong time. You were expecting something to happen when you pushed all those buttons, but nothing happened. Ultimately, your bad decisions and choices caused the destruction of the entire vessel, and her crew, not to mention loosing your children's college funds in a messy divorce.
Wait. What?
This review keeps getting away from me. Let me focus:
You program your moves, a la Robo Rally, while a CD plays a prerecorded track of incoming threats to your little sitting-duck class ship that just warped into this quadrant for a "quicky" sector scan. Suddenly, like George Lucas making a surprise appearance at a screening of The Force Awakens, everything and everyone wants to kill you. But, if you and your friends can communicate effectively, and plan accordingly, you might survive long enough for the automatic warp drive to zap you out of here in ten, real-time, minutes.
But that's misleading. The CD runs for 10 minutes while you run here and there, try to allocate energy, fire weapons, and keep the internal and external threats managed. But your doing it all by programming your moves and actions with cards that aren't revealed until the CD stops, and you painstakingly recreate the event step-by-step. That process takes a lot longer than 10 minutes, and it's here that you realized you messed up: you didn't put down the right card, two of you tried to use the one-man lift in the same section, and you left Becky's telephone number in your jacket pocket. 
This would have been amazing if it all could actually happen in "real-time." But the "do-over" sense of recreating the actual event kills the mood.
Space Cadets is a game that does this better, but with fiddly, dexterity based mini-games. And both games are a PAIN to teach.
If you have the same group playing over and over, you can actually get good at Space Alert, but to convince someone to finish a day of mind-numbing employment and then come over to your house to PLAY A GAME which simulates frustration and incompetence, it's a tough sell.
I really wanted to love it. I really did. But like your feelings for the red-head in accounting, some things are better left on the shelf.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
What was I thinking when I got this? Well, I like Science-Fiction, the game is pretty and the new edition with translucent cubes made me finally pull the trigger, but there were reasons for not having bought it earlier. I'm not particularly fond of the cooperative genre, and I usually dislike games where the players simultaneously program their whole series of actions for the round. Here, they program the entire game! And they do it collectively to solve an unforgiving puzzle... in ten minutes!

The game is not easy and includes a tutorial that takes the players through several lessons, test runs and simulations to prepare them for the real thing. A problem with this is that you have to play always with the same group if you expect to have a good performance. Besides, as it often happens with Vlaada's designs, the game scales poorly with the number of players and requires the use of dummies with two or three. It is worse than usual, because the players must collectively control these so-called androids in the same ten minutes!

The saddest thing for me is that obviously a lot of effort was put into the design, and it might have been a decent game if it was supposed to be played in the traditional turn-based way. Also, I came to the conclusion that the real-time playing was false advertising, as you don't actually play the game in those ten minutes, in the sense that you perform actions and see their results. You just program them, and if not for some new info and instructions given by the CD, the game would be nothing more than a puzzle with a time limit.

RATING: 8
What makes Space Alert so great is that the real time gameplay eliminates two gaming pratfalls: 1) Analysis Paralysis - a player simply cannot take forever on their turn, debating how to maximize it to the fullest. 2) Quarterbacking - one of the problems I have with Pandemic is that an extremely vocal/dominant player can simply orchestrate other players' turns for them, certain players can just tune out if they want to while the others move their piece for them because there are more clearly defined "correct moves."  Space Alert is just chaos!  It's quick, it's challenging, it's utterly unique.

I like the way that, more than most other cooperatives, it really forces players to work together. Unfortunately, it's one of those things that given sufficient time you could easily win every time, so it's really just a race against the clock (and possibly the cards in your hand). It fits the theme well, but I'd rather play something else. It is very satisfying to see the plan come together, but it's mostly just chaotic. There's not much in the way of real strategy here, just fast-paced, chaotic teamwork. Not really my thing.

First time out we played [b]TEN[/b] 5-player games in a row (don't recall ever doing that before!), starting off with the intro games and moving up.  The self training approach was very nicely done.

Highly recommend this game if you are into coop games.  It is a cross between [GAMEID=18] and [GAMEID=12350], in the [GAMEID=31481] universe!

RATING: 8
So much more than a mere game—this is an amazing artifact, a tool, in the exercise of teamwork, planning, and unforeseen consequences.  Seriously—it could be used for real-life team training.

But before you wave off because you're worried you'll be forced to play this with your boss at a corporate retreat, know that it's tense, trying (in the good way) and most of all fun.  The theme is hard-SF enough to feel serious, with just a dusting of levity that never becomes cartoony (something that turns me off of [i]Galaxy Trucker[/i]).

I was worried the audio track would be goofy and kitschy, but instead I found it to be brilliantly immersive.

RATING: 7
This is really pretty brilliant--the idea is to make a cooperative game where the difficult part is the cooperation itself.  It eliminates a lot of problems with cooperative games in general--the one-player issue, the artificiality of the challenges, and the boring repitition of many co-ops.  Instead, it's all about cooperative negotiation.

However, the negotiation is pretty chaotic and it can be very frustrating to try to form a plan with four other people at once while they are also working with each other.  It's stressful and kind of exhausting.  (1 play)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
--Sep 24, 2011--

After 2 plays:
If this game ended after the soundtrack stopped, I'd rate it several points higher. Sadly, once you're done "playing," you have to go through the entire game a second time and re-enact the whole thing "for reals this time." The last half of the experience is just watching as everybody reveals a card, one person pushes a few cubes around on the board, and then you reveal cards again, push more cubes, repeat until you've done it a dozen times. This game would be _perfect_ for couch co-op on the Xbox or something. You can yell at each other for 10 minutes while the timer ticks down, and then it zips through all the mechanical cube pushing and laser shooting and damage dealing in one or two minutes. I don't know that it would be great for me this way either (I'm only ok at best on the real-time portion), but I'd be willing to play it a few more times and find out. With the boring play-it-again-Sam portion tacked on, I don't know that I'd give it another chance.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A very original cooperative game.  The implementation of a CD audio track to put pressure on players, combined with keeping track of hidden actions, makes this game really tense and exciting.  It seems simple at first glance, but wait until the reactor runs out of energy and you find yourself firing blanks at the incoming aliens!

Gets better with more plays.  If you don't have a group that is willing to commit to playing it over and over in a relatively short time period (say, 10 games over a couple months), then it might not be for you.  But it's worth the time commitment, imho.

Purchased from BGGer "Howitzer_120mm" with custom role nameplates, June 2012.

RATING: 8.5
I love this game. This is one of few real time mechanics that I play and I think that it is amazing. The game can be considered as the internal story of what is happening on Galaxy Trucker, I have to say that the cover doesn't help a lot to do the connection, in Space Alert it seems that you are at the Enterprise or something like that, but when you see the board, the weapons and the engines, the theme, everything connects with Galaxy Trucker.
Having said that, the key of the game is the real time system: how well the players are able to organize themselves, how they adapt to following orders, yes, eventually someone will have to take the decisions and you will have to obey (or they will have to follow your commands)... I totally understand that this game is not for every players. A lot of gamers want to analyze the situation, what options do they have and then take the best option. I totally get that because I also like that kind of game, but the excitement of trying to make all those decisions in a limited time, being sure that no one will take the lift at the same time than you are going to use it, to combine the energy reload before using it, or after using it, taking care of the internal threat when the remaining players have already planned their first 6 moves and the alien is going to blow the ship by turn 7... all those things, for me, are amazing. Having said that, yes there are many hard times, the ship is going to blow up many, many times, you are going to feel depressed eventually, but when everything comes into place and there are no big mistakes and you can play 2 or 3 games without losing, the game is amazing.
Positive:
1. The real time mechanic
2. The theme and how thematic everything is
3. Easy to explain
4. Quick to play, the audio tape takes around 10 minutes. Then the resolution phase another 20 or so
5. Real feeling of success when you survive
Negative:
1. It is very difficult. Even without using the yellow cards, do not say anything about the red ones...
2. Every player needs to be focused. It is very easy to make a mistake by playing an movement to the right when you have to move left. Trust me, VERY easy
3. It may be difficult finding a group where all players enjoy the game
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
